## v2.5.51 learnings (pseat scaling - a fix without a free reference point)

- **Not every anchor-constant fix gets a provably-exact reference point.** T5's Edge fix (`-16*_ek`) was exact at the reference because `_ek` was a NEW factor defaulting to 1. Here, the raw `+8`/`+6` was ALREADY baked in at some unknown historical `l2pub.s`; multiplying by the CURRENT `l2pub.s` only preserves the reference to within however close that historical value happened to be to today's computed one. When that's the situation: compute the actual reference-scale value from first principles (here: L2 SOLO mode's `pubBox()`/`baseS` formula), quantify the resulting discrepancy in px, and present it honestly rather than claiming false precision.
- **A function's default/most-common mode matters more than its edge cases when picking a verification reference.** L2 has SOLO (default, no neighbours folded) and COLUMN (1-3 wide, opt-in) modes with WILDLY different `l2pub.s` (≈1.13 vs ≈0.36 at the same window width) - verify against whichever the user actually experiences by default, and note the other mode's number honestly rather than silently ignoring it.
- **A comment claiming symmetry ("mirrors Edge") can be aspirational rather than descriptive - verify, don't inherit trust.** Edge's OWN L2 position has zero raw-pixel arithmetic; pseat's comment claimed to mirror it while actually diverging from that exact pattern. Re-derive from the CODE, not the comment describing it.

## v2.5.52 learnings (aside-aware dock width; a caught patch-script slip)

- **A position formula that unconditionally reserves space for a conditionally-rendered sibling wastes that space whenever the sibling is absent.** v2.5.50 gave Crypt/Library/Ash/Hand/Pool a `var(--dock-log-w)+8px` prefix unconditionally, but `#dockLog` itself only ever displays when `body.aside-collapsed` - so the reserved space sat empty (and, post-reorder, visibly at the FRONT of the row) whenever the aside was expanded. When adding a shift/prefix to accommodate a sibling, check that sibling's OWN visibility condition and gate the shift the same way - or better, as here, give the freed space to something that can use it.
- **Match an existing codebase pattern (`#dockLog`'s own base-hidden/aside-collapsed-shown split) rather than inventing a new one.** The fix mirrors that exact structure (base = simpler/no-shift, `body.aside-collapsed` override = the shift), keeping the two related concerns (log's own visibility, everyone else's position) expressed the same way.
- **A shared-budget split (`avail = total - fixed`) generalizes cleanly to "N-way sharing becomes 1-way sharing when N-1 participants are absent"** - `updateDockScale()` needed one new `if(asideCollapsed)` branch, no restructuring of the `avail` computation itself (which remains correct regardless of how many flexible elements ultimately consume it).
- **Verify a multi-edit CSS/JS patch by grep-checking the RESULT structure, not just the gate outputs.** A slipped anchor in the patch script left a redundant, confusing extra `#z-pool{display:block;}` rule in the file after the atomic-edit step (gates would NOT have caught this - it was syntactically valid, harmless-looking CSS, just wrong to have shipped). Caught by eyeballing the diff area with `grep -n` before running the gates, removed with a targeted `str_replace`, and never reached a delivered version. Habit worth keeping: after any patch that inserts new blocks (not just single-line edits), grep the affected region once more to visually confirm only the intended rules exist.

## v2.5.53 learnings (the other half of the anchor-math class; offsetWidth ignores transform)

- **`transform-origin` anchor-math has TWO independent drift sources, and fixing only the additive offset's scaling isn't the whole fix.** Any `left`/`top` value combines with the element's OWN (unscaled) dimensions at the transform-origin to determine the pivot point; if the origin isn't `0 0`, the element's natural size contributes to where that pivot lands, and that contribution needs its own compensation (subtracted as a flat constant, NOT scaled) alongside scaling the gap term. v2.5.51 fixed the gap; v2.5.53 fixed the pivot. When an anchor-math bug persists after a first attempt, re-derive from `transform-origin` first, not just from "which numbers were unscaled."
- **`offsetWidth`/`offsetHeight` reflect an element's own LAYOUT box, unaffected by any CSS `transform` currently applied to it (transforms are a paint-time operation, not a layout one).** This makes them safe to read for anchor-compensation math regardless of what scale was set on a PREVIOUS render - no need to reset the transform before measuring.
- **When an element's size varies by content (here: seat-letter length) rather than being a known constant, measure it live rather than assuming a fixed number** - unlike Edge's KNOWN 32px diameter (a hardcoded halving sufficed), pseat's width depends on the seat label, so `pst.offsetWidth||fallback` is the correct generalization of the same anchor-compensation pattern.
- **A user re-reporting the SAME symptom after a fix means the fix addressed a REAL but INCOMPLETE cause - re-derive from first principles rather than tweaking the same formula further.** Re-deriving the full pivot equation (rather than adjusting constants by feel) is what surfaced the missing `-naturalWidth` term and let a numeric harness prove the corrected version keeps BOTH edges proportional, not just plausible.

## v2.5.54 learnings (anchor the edge the spec references)

- **When an alignment spec names a specific edge, make THAT edge the transform-origin - drift becomes impossible by construction, and compensation terms retire.** The pseat saga needed three attempts precisely because the first two kept the WRONG anchor (right edge / far-corner pivot) and compensated around it: v2.5.51 scaled the gap term, v2.5.53 measured-and-subtracted the natural width. Flipping to `transform-origin:0 0` with `left = target` made the left edge the pivot itself - both compensations deleted, net code SHORTER than before the saga began. General rule: before adding compensation math to anchor arithmetic, ask whether changing the origin eliminates the need for it entirely.
- **A `literal/scale` offset inside a scaled wrapper is scale-invariant on screen - and the codebase already documents these ("offset stays literal").** The Pool label's `10/l2pub.s` inside `scale(l2pub.s)` puts the P at a constant 10px from the zone edge at every size; aligning a SCREEN-positioned sibling to it therefore takes a literal `+10`, not a scaled one. When aligning across the wrapper boundary, first classify each side as literal-on-screen or scaling-on-screen.
- **The user's eyeball estimate can be a precision instrument - check it against the code before rounding it away.** "Kanske 10-15 pixlar" turned out to be EXACTLY the label's documented literal 10px offset; taking the estimate seriously and grepping for its source produced the exact spec instead of a tuned approximation.

## v2.5.55 learnings (ratio redistribution)

- **A well-factored shared-budget system makes "resize A, give it to B" a two-number change.** Because Hand/Log widths flow through CSS variables from ONE ratio in ONE function, moving 30% of Log to Hand touched only the two baseline constants (+ the comments/CSS defaults naming them) - the clamp, every position formula, and the aside-expanded branch all adapted automatically. Preserving the TOTAL (960) is the key invariant to check: it keeps the overflow-clamp behaviour byte-for-byte equivalent.

## v2.5.56 learnings (the small backlog batch)

- **A backlog item scoped to one surface ("L4 give hit-test") may have siblings on every parallel surface - grep the shared mechanism, not the named symptom.** The kid-hit problem lived in THREE give paths (L4 wrap, pane/mat surfaces, visit-view drag); one shared helper (`pubTopHostEl`) fixed all three, where a literal reading of the backlog entry would have patched one.
- **Protocol fields added for error-targeting should degrade gracefully across version drift**: the client prefers the cid-targeted clear but falls back to the old wholesale clear when the field is absent (old server) - never leave a guard leaking because the peer is one version behind.
- **A machine-check added to close a documented gap should be EXPECTED to fail on first run - that failure is the deliverable.** The new §3 lint immediately surfaced three stale doc fields (hostToken/players/v) that had sat unverifiable since the doc was written. Verify against the live code before deciding which side to correct (the code genuinely never read them - the doc was aspirational), then correct the DOC per the lint doctrine.
- **Brace-walking verb branches out of raw source text (backlog option b) matches Function.toString() extraction perfectly for lint purposes** - same field-regex family, zero runtime risk, and it reuses the doc's existing table parser untouched.

## v2.5.57–60 learnings (the backlog sweep, part 2)

- **A well-written backlog entry is executable months later**: 2.1's "Why deferred" carried the exact first step (read renderL2's pane block - the previous session's grep filter had excluded it) and its How was implementable almost verbatim. Writing deferral notes AS IF for a stranger pays off precisely when it matters.
- **A generic router with value-typed verbs extends without protocol change** - the server's `act: String(m.act).slice(0,16)` routing meant seven new ctrl verbs needed ZERO server edits, and the field-lint proves it (ctrl's field list unchanged). Design routers around opaque verb values, not enumerated fields.
- **Optimism over FULL-snapshot sync is self-healing by construction**: apply locally, re-render, and the authoritative push overwrites everything - corrections GLIDE thanks to the reconcile. Optimism over TWO-HOP confirm flows (recall) must instead be REVERSIBLE-visual-only: dim at once, spawn only on confirm, un-dim on err/deny - state changes stay confirmation-gated so an offline holder can never cost the card.
- **Debounces exist for a reason - know which reason before bypassing**: schedulePush's 600ms serves typing-speed LOCAL edits; a cross-board round-trip someone is actively waiting on deserves `pushNow()`. Name the immediate variant, don't lower the debounce.
- **Impersonation-based dev tools must restore in `finally`**: elysiumPubPreview temporarily swaps `net.boards[mySeat()]` to force the opponent render branch - the try/finally restore guarantees a render throw can never leak fake state into the live game.
- **When reusing panels, exit paths KEEP their teardown**: renderL2's in-loop wipe was the bug; clearL2's on-exit wipe is correct. Reuse scopes to a mode's lifetime, not across mode boundaries.

## v2.5.61–62 learnings

- **A dedup rule enforced on one join path but not its sibling is a latent identity bug** - and the lookups that CONSUME names must match the rule that ADMITS them (case-insensitive admit + case-sensitive lookup = wrong-record resolution). Fix admits and lookups as one coordinated set, never one side.
- **"Both parties can end the arrangement" is a cleaner interaction contract than one-sided rights** - Johan's Give back design turned 2.4.a's thought-heavy gating question into a symmetric pair (owner: Take back, holder: Give back), and the ENTIRE implementation rode existing machinery (give->gave online, giveHot hotseat) because the gave handler was already zone-agnostic. Check the existing verbs' assumptions before building new ones - the answer may already be built.
- **Routing-only server verbs need message-capture to test, and a 20-line WS-frame decoder on a fake socket is all it takes** - recSock() decodes coalesced server frames into a msgs array, making give/recall/recalled assertable end-to-end in the same harness that already drove create/join. The verb family that once shipped four latent bugs now has a behaviour lock, added the same day the family was touched three times.
- **A docs diet is a straight verbatim cut, not a rewrite** - moving pre-today journal entries to an archive file with a pointer costs minutes and made every future session-start ritual ~85% cheaper on the journal; summarising or editing during the move would have risked the historical record for no gain.

## v2.5.63 learnings (the path-badge leak)

- **A data field can mean different things per record KIND - a shared accessor must not flatten that distinction.** KRCG's `path` field means "follows this path" on crypt cards but "REQUIRES this path" on library cards; `effPath()` returns it for both, so every consumer needs the kind filter that matches its semantics. `detectPath()` had it; the badge site didn't - when adding a consumer of a shared accessor, check what filters the EXISTING consumers apply and why.
- **An icon-load error handler that hides only the <img> leaves the decorated CONTAINER visible** - if the badge has its own background/ring styling, the error path must hide the whole badge, not just the image inside it. Grep `addEventListener('error'` sites for this pattern when a "empty ring/box" symptom is reported.
- **The user's precise wording encodes the diagnosis**: "har ELLER KRÄVER en Path" distinguished printed-follower paths from requirement fields in one phrase - reading the report literally pointed straight at the kind-semantics split.

## v2.5.64 learnings (badge only what the card doesn't already say)

- **UI indicators should mark DELTAS from what's already visible, not restate the artefact** - a printed path is on the card art; badging it is redundancy, while a CHOSEN path is invisible without the badge. When adding an indicator, ask "what does the viewer NOT already see?"
- **Before simplifying an accessor call to a raw field, verify the states are mutually exclusive by construction** - c.path alone is exact here only because the Follow Path menu gates itself on !_krcgPath; that gate is what makes the simplification safe rather than approximate. Cite the gate in the comment so a future menu change knows what it would break.
- **Scope a semantic change to the one consumer it concerns**: effPath()/detectPath() (globe icon, majority detection) still correctly include printed paths - only the BADGE's semantics changed. One accessor, several consumers, per-consumer semantics.

## v2.5.65 learnings (animation curve surgery)

- **"X shows too briefly" in a multi-phase animation is usually a KEYFRAME-PERCENTAGE problem, not a duration problem** - extending the total slows every phase (side effect on the parts that already felt right); moving the boundary percentage between phases grows exactly the window the user named. Decompose the animation into its phases (here: flip-in 0-50%, front hold 50-76%, fade 76-100%) and edit the one boundary.
- **Percentage-based keyframes compose for free with duration-scaling controls** (conv.animSpeed / the BASE table): a curve reshape needs zero JS changes and inherits the user's speed preference automatically.
- **Quantify the change against the user's number**: "kanske 30%" -> 26%->34% of 1.15s = +30.8%, stated in the code comment so the calibration survives.

## v2.5.66 learnings (why the v2.5.65 nudge was imperceptible)

- **What reads as "the face is showing" is the FLAT, STATIC phase - a slowly-settling rotation does not count perceptually.** v2.5.65 moved the fade boundary inside a rotation that spanned the whole second half, so nothing readable changed. When a user says an animation phase is too short, first check whether that phase EXISTS as a static segment at all - creating one beats stretching boundaries around motion.
- **"Keep phase X's length, grow phase Y" across a total-duration change = recompute percentages to preserve ABSOLUTE times**: newPct = oldMs/newTotal (38.3%/76.7% of 1.5s == 50%/100% of 1.15s). State both the percentages and the ms in the comment - the ms are the spec, the percentages are the encoding.
- **When a per-kind duration table exists (BASE), a CSS duration change must update it in the same patch** or the user's speed-scaling setting silently reverts the CSS value.

## v2.5.67 learnings (iterating animation feel with the user)

- **Preserving a phase's absolute speed is a spec choice, not a law - re-ask when the user's next report contradicts the feel.** v2.5.66 faithfully kept the back+flip at its historical tempo per the then-spec; Johan's next test showed the EVEN SPLIT itself was the problem. Animation feel converges by iteration: each round, restate the phase table in ms and let the user point at the phase to shrink/grow.
- **Once an animation is decomposed into named phases with one keyframe-percentage each, every future adjustment is a one-number change** - the v2.5.66 restructure (rotation completing early + a static segment) is what made this v2.5.67 reallocation a two-line edit, and makes the NEXT tweak trivial too. Structure first, then tune.

## v2.5.68 learnings (per-segment easing demands symmetric motion segments)

- **CSS `animation-timing-function` applies PER KEYFRAME SEGMENT - uneven segment lengths inside one continuous motion produce visible speed hitches.** v2.5.67's 0-20/20-40/40-100 split made the rotation decelerate, re-accelerate and decelerate with mismatched curve shapes; the fix is Johan's principle: keep the MOTION's segments symmetric (mirror halves, 25/50), and park ALL added viewing time in a zero-motion segment AFTER the motion completes - a static segment has no easing artefacts by definition.
- **The user's "hackar till" (it hitches) is a per-segment-easing fingerprint** when the keyframe percentages were recently made uneven - check the segment table before suspecting performance.
- **Three feel-iterations (v2.5.65-68) converged because each round restated the phase table in ms and let Johan point at what to change** - the final spec came FROM him in exact numbers (500 split 50/50, +300 before fade), which is the natural end state of this kind of loop: the structure work (phases as single percentages) made every iteration a minutes-long edit.

## v2.5.69 learnings (knowing when to revert)

- **A feel-iteration loop that hasn't converged after a few rounds should offer FULL REVERT as a first-class outcome, not a failure** - the original had survived long real use (its feel was proven); reverting restores that baseline while the journal keeps every intermediate insight (phase anatomy, symmetric-segment rule, static-hold principle) ready for a cheaper second attempt later.
- **Revert to the DOCUMENTED original, character-exact, and grep-verify each restored value** - the patch history carried the pre-iteration text verbatim, making the revert provable rather than approximate.

## v2.5.72 learnings (the branch merge)

- **Two branches that both "continued" from one point merge cleanly when their journals are good** - B's session entries functioned as an executable merge map (exact functions, sites, counts), and fork verification is cheap: pre-fork markers PRESENT in both + post-fork markers ABSENT crosswise proves the topology before any code moves.
- **A user-reported "regression" may be a fix living in a PARALLEL timeline** - the unlock guard existed in B while A concluded "never existed"; both were right within their branch. When memory and code disagree, ask which BUILD the memory comes from before concluding.
- **Infrastructure branch × calibration branch is the ideal merge**: B built the tuning table (FX_TIMING), A found the perfect values - expressing A's result in B's table was provable in Node to 1 ms per phase boundary, and the JS swap derivation landed on the same 575 ms automatically.
- **Slice-transplant beats patch-replay for additive features; keep the branch with surgical rewires as base** - and write-after-loop discipline saved the merge twice (assert-aborts caught a structural error and a false assumption before any bytes hit disk).
- **Before "restructuring" anything during a merge, check whether the structure already exists** - the updateBadges split was pre-fork; B's journal described USE, not creation, and the planned surgery collapsed into a trivial block insert.
- **A design decision made LATER in one branch governs the other branch's parallel feature** (chosen-only paths applied to B's remote badges, veto-flagged) - reconcile by principle, not by timestamp of implementation.
- **Bytes ≠ chars in UTF-8 baselines**: assert against len(text) in the same unit you measured (an 888523-byte file is 886087 chars) - a mismatched-unit assert reads like corruption when the file is actually fine.

## v2.5.73 learnings (the online sweep: whitelist drift, fx coupling, modal stranding)

- **`sanitizePub` is the server-side twin of the state.X serialization rule — any new `buildPub` field must be added to the whitelist in the SAME patch, or the feature works in hotseat and silently dies online.** Hotseat pubs go straight into `net.boards` and never pass the server; online pubs pass `sanitizePub`, a strict whitelist that predated the token family. Six fields had accumulated in buildPub without ever being relayed (`tokens`, `clan`, card `actSt`/`_avSeq`/`path`/`sup`) — each shipped with a hotseat-verified feature whose online half was dead on arrival (Edge/globe sync, the L4 clan icon, remote path/sup badges, the Played tab's cross-seat order). The tell in a bug report: "works offline, invisible online" with an intact client chain. Grep `sanitizePub` whenever buildPub grows; the protocol doc's §5 prose lists the intended pub surface and is the contract to sync against.
- **The fx verb and the board push must travel together.** `cardFx` broadcast the clone IMMEDIATELY while the board sat in schedulePush's 600 ms debounce — opponents watched an animation for a card that hadn't materialised. Fix pattern (extends the v2.5.59 pushNow doctrine): call `pushNow()` right after the fx mpSend, inside the same broadcast gate. Never lower the debounce — it exists for counter bursts, which reach the wire via the log→scheduleSave chain and must keep coalescing.
- **`openOverlay()` is EXCLUSIVE by design (hides every other modal + nulls labDuo) — any flow reachable from a lobby needs a return ticket, or Close strands the player on the welcome screen.** The `lobbyReturn` pattern: the displacing opener stamps which lobby it displaced; consumers (`closeToLobby`, the deck lab's beside-branch) restore-and-consume; `openOverlay` carries a stale-guard (any modal outside the deck-flow forfeits the ticket, so a Settings detour can never resurrect a ghost lobby); restoration validates liveness (`net.lobby`) and degrades to a plain close. Test such state machines by grabbing the functions VERBATIM out of the patched file and running them under DOM stubs — the 7-sequence harness caught nothing this time precisely because the transitions were designed on paper first, and now regression-locks them.
- **A pub field with zero receive-side consumers is a liability, not a freebie** — `buildPub().anchor` (the local selection anchor) travelled nowhere (the whitelist ate it) and meant nothing to opponents; removing it keeps buildPub an exact map of what the table actually sees. When auditing sync, diff buildPub's fields against BOTH sanitizePub and the receive-side consumers; a field missing from either side is a bug in one direction or dead weight in the other.


## v2.5.74 learnings (the fx relay's enum hole; transport follows the target)

- **The whitelist-drift class has (at least) two members: `sanitizePub` AND the `fx` relay's kind-enum + field list.** Same fingerprint ("works offline, invisible online"), same cause (a server-side allowlist that predated client growth), same audit: when a clone/effect exists in hotseat but not online, grep the server handler for BOTH the enum and the relayed field set before touching the client. The protocol doc's §7/§8 rows are executable contracts — twice now the doc was AHEAD of the reference server (§5 pub prose, §8 fx fields); diff handlers against their doc rows, not just against the client.
- **Cosmetic relays can relax privacy invariants where state relays cannot — but scope it to one provable case.** fxCardInfo's "face-down never carries a name" stays right in general; the conceal-flip is the one case where the name was public a frame earlier AND the remote animation is meaningless without it (back→back). The exception lives at exactly two sites (client mpSend term, server post-fxCard override), each commented with the rationale + "fx is cosmetic; no state trusts it".
- **Socket security follows the TARGET, not the page.** Mixed-content law only binds upward (https page ⇒ wss); a file:// or http page may freely open wss. Deriving wss-vs-ws from `location.protocol` alone silently forbids the local-client→tunnel topology; sniffing an explicit pasted scheme (and remembering it in `net.sec` for reconnects + storage) enables it with zero change for every existing caller — bare hosts keep page-protocol behaviour by construction.
- **Per-origin storage is a design constraint to route around, not to fight:** the "prepared guest" answer keeps the user AT their stable origin (local file/localhost) and moves only the socket — never try to move browser storage across origins (impossible by same-origin policy). Files on disk, VDB links, and data baked into the client (precons from the card DB) are the origin-independent transports.

## v2.5.75 learnings (the dock pin machine; eval-scope harness rule; the chip→background-click iteration)

- **Gate the auto-closers, centralize the release.** The dock pin works by gating every AUTO-close path on `!dockPinned` — which makes any `setL2Dock(false)` that actually EXECUTES legitimate by construction, so the pin release can live in ONE place (setL2Dock's close branch) and every explicit closer (chip, menu, code) releases it for free. Inverting this (checking the pin inside setL2Dock) would have forced every explicit closer to carry an override flag.
- **One-shot flags for mode transitions:** `drag.dockCloseNow` triggers the L4 defer-exit on the first move and is consumed immediately — a deliberate deep re-entry (yd ≥ H−36) can still reopen the dock without being instantly re-closed. Any "do X once at the start of a gesture, but let the per-move rules rule afterwards" belongs in a one-shot on the gesture object, not in a standing condition.
- **The reopen sliver explains why start-close never flickers:** the dock's hover-open zone is only the bottommost 36 px (H−36), while cards' grab points sit well above it — so 'close' mode's instant exit doesn't collide with the reopen rule in practice. The edge case (grabbing inside the sliver itself) resolves to net-open, which is self-consistent: the sliver IS the open-the-dock zone.
- **eval scope rule for verbatim harnesses:** `function` declarations in a direct eval leak to the surrounding variable environment, but `let`/`const` are scoped to THAT eval call — a `let`+`function` pair grabbed from the file must be eval'd in ONE combined eval (exactly as they cohabit the module scope in the file), with getters exported from inside the same eval for the test to read the `let`.
- **Settings hints should name the deliberate exceptions.** The dockDrag row says outright that the L2/L3 hand fan always rides out the drag (it IS the hand UI there) and that the pin overrides both modes — an honest hint prevents the "setting doesn't work" bug report the omission would earn.
- **A large background element as a click target needs `e.target===el`, not `closest()`.** `#l2dockbg` sits BEHIND the dock's piles/cards (lower z-index) — clicks on those never reach it — but any future child appended inside it (without its own `stopPropagation`) would bubble up and could mis-trigger the parent's handler under a `closest()`-style check. An exact-target equality check is the correct guard for "the background itself, not a descendant."
- **Any element that becomes independently clickable must be re-audited against existing "don't spawn a marquee / don't clear selection" exclusion lists.** `#l2dockbg` had no click meaning before, so it was absent from the T3c exclusion `closest()` list; giving it a click handler without adding it there would have let every pin-click also fire a zero-size marquee. The lesson generalizes: a click-exclusion list is a contract that every future click-bearing element must join, not a one-time list.
- **`box-shadow` sidesteps CSS specificity fights that overriding an already-set property can't.** `#l2dockbg`'s base display rules carry TWO id selectors each (`#board`+`#l2dockbg`) plus several classes; a lower-specificity `.pinned` variant could never win a `border-top-color` override without matching that weight. Adding `box-shadow` instead — a property the base rules never touch — sidesteps the fight entirely, since specificity conflicts are resolved per-property, not per-rule. When a state-variant class needs to change a property the base rule already sets, either match/exceed the base rule's specificity or, if visually equivalent, pick a different property the base rule doesn't own.
- **A UI micro-iteration can happen entirely within one delivered version.** The pin went chip(📌) → chip(⌃) → background-click across three back-to-back turns, all still "v2.5.75," at the person's explicit "don't document this yet, we're just testing" instruction each time. The lesson: when a person defers documentation for rapid iteration, the FIRST post-settling implementation turn is responsible for reconciling every doc that described the abandoned intermediate states — not just adding a new delta on top of stale text.

## v2.5.76 learnings (a "fixed elsewhere" parity gap is a distinct bug class; two CSS root-causes hiding behind cosmetic symptoms)

- **A parity fix that only touches ONE branch of an `if(l4on()){…}else{…}` leaves the other branch exactly as broken as before, even when both branches looked like they were "the same feature."** v2.5.75 correctly gave L4 hand drags a defer-until-leaves mechanic (`l4DockDefer`), but the sibling `else` branch (real L3) still closed the dock and jumped to felt scale unconditionally — a second, independent bug with the same visible symptom class ("dock closes too early") that a parity audit should have caught at the time. When fixing "L4 does X wrong," always check whether the non-L4 sibling branch handling the SAME gesture has the SAME gap — `l3on()` is true during L4 too, which is exactly why these two paths are so easy to conflate and so easy to under-audit.
- **A value computed once at gesture-start and never revisited is a bug waiting for any state that can change mid-gesture.** The library/crypt ghost's scale was set once at drag start from `l2pub.s`/`dockS`, but the dock can open/close mid-drag (the existing per-move hysteresis already toggles it) — so the ghost's visual size silently stopped matching the dock's real state the moment that toggle fired later in the same gesture. The fix pattern: extract the computation into a named function and re-call it on every tick that could observe a state change, not just at the gesture's first frame.
- **Unicode "emoji-presentation-by-default" characters (`Emoji_Presentation=Yes`) render larger/coloured even under identical CSS to their siblings.** U+2757 vs U+23F1/U+27F2/U+2316 in the SAME `.btn` class looked mismatched in size purely because of the character's own default presentation, not the stylesheet. A trailing U+FE0E (text variation selector) forces the text glyph without touching CSS — the fix belongs in the character, not the rule, whenever a symbol button looks "bigger" than its siblings despite sharing every CSS property.
- **A guessed-duration `setTimeout` standing in for "a CSS transition finished" is a race, not a fix, even when the guess comfortably exceeds the transition's declared duration.** `setAside`'s 200ms timeout (vs. the aside's declared 140ms transition) usually won the race, but "usually" is exactly the signature of Johan's report ("doesn't seem fully solved" — it self-healed on an unrelated click, meaning the correction eventually landed via SOME resize, just not reliably the scheduled one). `transitionend` (gated on the specific `propertyName`) is the actual completion signal and removes the race entirely; keep a generous timeout only as a backstop for the transition-never-fires edge case (hidden ancestor, reduced-motion).
- **Two adjacent CSS rules that both touch a shared property can combine in a way that's invisible at one call site's ambient styling and glaring at another's.** `.clansym{margin-right:0.18em}` (EM, scales with ambient font-size) and `.clanbadge img{margin-left:2px}` (fixed px) both apply to the same badge icon at every one of `clanSymBadge()`'s three call sites — the resulting off-centre push is proportional to the SURROUNDING font-size, so it was imperceptible in a ~14px settings row and obvious in the clone caption's 27px context. The generalizable check: when a shared component "looks fine everywhere except one place," diff what ELSE is different about that one place (here: ambient font-size feeding an EM value) before assuming the component itself needs a per-site override — the correct fix is usually to make the shared rule stop depending on ambient context (explicit margin shorthand zeroing the sides the class doesn't intend to own), not to patch the one call site that happened to expose it.
- **A colour/identity lookup keyed on a parameter that one call site always sends as a sentinel value silently degrades ONLY at that call site.** `saySend`'s hotseat branch always passes `seat=0` to `showSay` (0 was never a meaningful seat number here — hotseat just never threaded a real one through), so any new feature keyed on that `seat` argument would work online (where the server fills in the real seat) and silently show nothing in hotseat. Resolving identity by NAME through `net.roster` (populated in both transports, exactly like `nameColor()` already does) sidesteps the sentinel entirely rather than requiring saySend's hotseat call site to be taught to pass a real seat.

## v2.5.77 hotfix learning (the defensive fallback shipped one call site too late)

- **Defending a lookup against a bad parameter doesn't help if a DIFFERENT parameter, one hop upstream, is the one actually wrong.** v2.5.76 correctly made `sayIdentity()` robust against `saySend`'s hardcoded `seat=0` by resolving identity via the `who` NAME through `net.roster` instead. But it never checked what `who` itself was in the hotseat call — `state.playerName`, which per the code's own existing comment (`clanLogName()`, line ~2244) is explicitly the SOLO-mode name, never populated into `net.roster`. The defensive redesign correctly routed around one bad input and then fed the function a second, un-noticed bad input from the very call site it was designed to protect. **The generalizable check: when hardening a lookup function against a known-bad argument, also open the exact call site sending that argument and read EVERY OTHER argument it passes — a local variable with a plausible name (`state.playerName` reads like "the player's name") can be the wrong concept for the context (solo vs. hotseat) despite compiling and running without error.**
- **`state.playerName` vs. `net.you` is a recurring solo/hotseat identity fork already documented at three other call sites (`clanLogName()`, the pool-oust log line, the stats row) — a fourth, brand-new call site (`saySend`) used the wrong one on first write despite the pattern being established elsewhere in the same file.** When adding a new "who is the current player" read, grep for existing `net.you||state.playerName` occurrences first and match the pattern, rather than reasoning about identity from scratch — this file has enough of that reasoning already recorded in its own code.
- **A live-test report phrased as a question ("does it only work online?") is often more precise than it sounds — take the phrasing literally as a diagnostic clue.** Johan's exact framing (works online, not hotseat) pointed directly at a solo/hotseat fork rather than a generic "the feature doesn't work" report, and grepping for the two transports' respective identity variables (`net.you` vs `state.playerName`) found the bug in under a dozen lines of investigation.

## v2.5.78 learning (a decorative pattern that works at one scale can fail when reused at another)

- **A "watermark behind a large surface" treatment (`.clansym-pool`: faint icon + tinted circle, both sized to the ELEMENT itself) doesn't transplant cleanly onto "decoration behind a much bigger neighbour."** `.clansym-pool` works on the pool globe because the circle IS the globe — the number sits centred inside a background that's exactly its own size. Reusing the same faint/translucent language as a backdrop behind an unrelated, much taller sibling (here: the 58px phrase line) buries both the circle and the icon under the visual weight of that sibling; nothing was technically broken, the composition itself was the wrong shape for the content. When adding a decorative circle+icon behind existing content, check whether the circle's job is "BE the primary visual" (watermark language is fine) or "sit ALONGSIDE unrelated bigger content" (needs to be an opaque, self-contained badge instead — `.clanbadge`/`clanSymBadge()` is the app's existing pattern for exactly that second case, already proven and already fixed for centring in v2.5.76).
- **Recolouring text to a variable (per-player) colour removes a piece of implicit contrast design the original fixed colour was quietly providing.** `.saywho`'s original `--brass` was hand-picked to read against the felt; once it became `col.hex` (six different hues, some pale/high-luminance like yellow), the SAME text-shadow that worked for one fixed tone stopped guaranteeing contrast for all six. `-webkit-text-stroke` added alongside the existing shadow (not replacing it) is the generalizable fix whenever a previously-fixed UI colour becomes player-variable: audit every reader relying on the old colour's contrast margin, not just the element being recoloured.

## v2.5.79 learning (a two-part complaint has an implicit "keep this part" the person didn't spell out)

- **"X and Y are both too small" does not mean "replace the X+Y composition" — it can mean "keep the composition, scale up its parts."** Johan's v2.5.78 report ("the icon and the name don't fill the ring, they're rather small... name could be separate from the icon") was read as "abandon the big-circle-behind-everything composition, split name and icon into a small side-by-side badge instead" — a bigger structural change than what he actually meant. His v2.5.79 correction ("the big circle was great! just the icon inside it was too small") shows the ACTUAL fix was much narrower: enlarge one child element's size/opacity, nothing else. **When a piece of visual feedback names multiple elements as "too small" together, the safer first hypothesis is uniform under-scaling of a composition the person otherwise likes — not a request to restructure the composition — and it's worth a quick clarifying read-back for an ambiguous aesthetic note before doing a structural rewrite, especially right after having ALREADY misjudged the shape of a fix once in the same feature.**
- **A revert should restore the exact prior structure (HTML shape + CSS selectors), not reconstruct an approximation of it from memory of "what it looked like."** Going back to the big-circle version meant re-adding the literal `.saycircle`/`.sayclan` elements/rules and the exact `showSay()` string-building code from the v2.5.76 patch (available verbatim in this session's own edit history), not re-deriving new markup that merely resembles the old design — keeps the size/opacity tuning this round scoped to a single, precisely-identified pair of numbers instead of re-introducing subtle structural drift.

## v2.5.80 learning (two sibling elements sized in different units against different reference frames are never guaranteed proportional)

- **A percentage size is only meaningful relative to ITS OWN containing block — two sibling elements sized as "90%" and a fixed "190px" are not proportional to EACH OTHER unless their containing blocks happen to match.** `.sayclan{width:92%}` and `.saycircle{width:190px}` are both absolutely-positioned children of `.sayb`, but `.sayb` is `white-space:nowrap` and auto-sizes to its TEXT content (the phrase) — so `.sayclan`'s 92% tracked the phrase's width, not the circle's fixed 190px. The two only looked matched by coincidence for phrases long enough to make `.sayb` wider than ~190px; short phrases (exactly what quick-phrases mostly are) broke the assumption invisibly. **The generalizable check: whenever two sibling elements are meant to be visually proportional (an icon meant to fill a circle, a fill meant to match a track), verify they're sized in the SAME unit against the SAME reference — if one is a fixed px value, the other should be too (or both should be percentages of a THIRD, shared, non-content-dependent parent), not "percentage of an auto-sizing ancestor" paired with "a fixed px sibling."**
- **v2.5.79's fix (bump the percentage and opacity) treated the symptom (icon looks small) without diagnosing the mechanism (percentage-of-the-wrong-box) — it happened to look better on whatever test phrase was tried, but Johan's report ("still only fills about half") on a real, probably-shorter phrase re-exposed the same unit mismatch at a new pair of numbers.** The lesson repeats a pattern from earlier this session (v2.5.76→77's `seat=0` sentinel, v2.5.78's misread of "small" as "wrong composition"): a plausible-looking numeric tweak can pass a quick visual check while the underlying mechanism stays broken — worth pausing to ask "why is this actually small" (here: inspect what `%` resolves against) before reaching for bigger numbers.
- **When a person says "same as before, just recoloured," the safest implementation is a literal diff against the pre-feature rule, not a fresh restyle that merely evokes the old look.** `-webkit-text-stroke` was a reasonable contrast addition in isolation, but it wasn't what was asked for this round and made the element read as changed even though its font-size never moved — restoring the EXACT original rule (verbatim, from this session's own v2.5.76 patch) and changing only the one dimension actually requested (colour) is the more conservative and more correct response to "make it like it was before."

## v2.5.81 learning (colour-coding the same identity twice on one surface can cancel itself out)

- **Applying the same colour signal to two overlapping/adjacent elements doesn't reinforce it — a same-hued foreground can visually merge into a same-hued background instead of standing out against it.** The ring already carries the player's colour (as a background gradient); tinting the name text sitting ON that ring the SAME colour reduces the very contrast that made the ring legible as a distinct shape in the first place — two applications of the identical colour signal compete rather than combine. The generalizable check when adding a colour-coded identity marker: if it's being layered onto (or right next to) another element ALREADY carrying that same colour, ask whether the new coat of colour is additive information or just a redundant, contrast-eating duplicate — often the more legible design keeps the colour on exactly ONE surface (background OR text, not both) and lets everything else stay neutral.

## v2.5.82 learning: a THIRD recurring bug class — derived-cache staleness with incomplete trigger coverage

Two existing recurring classes are already documented: "L4 dispatch chain drift" (a render/dispatch chain missing an `'l4'` branch) and "a new `state.X` field must be added to both serializeGame/restoreGame AND snapshot/restoreSnap." This session surfaced a third, distinct pattern, worth naming for future audits:

- **A derived value cached in `state.X` and refreshed only by explicit `updateX()` calls at specific mutation sites is only as complete as the audit of EVERY way `X`'s underlying inputs can change.** `state.myClan` is 100% derivable from `state.zones.ready` + each card's `faceDown`/`kind`/`name` — but it's cached (not recomputed on every render) for performance, and only two conceptual triggers were ever wired: a zone change (`move()`) and playing a card (`tagPlayed()`). Toggling `faceDown` IN PLACE (no zone change) is a third, independent way the SAME inputs change, and it was never wired at all — not once, but at five separate call sites that all perform the identical mutation (`c.faceDown=!c.faceDown`) through different user gestures (menu, double-click, group actions, online mirror). **When a value is cached from a mutable set of inputs, the reliable audit isn't "does every place that RECOMPUTES it read the current inputs correctly" — it's "does every place that MUTATES one of the inputs also trigger a recompute."** Grep every assignment to the input field(s) (here: `c.faceDown=`) and cross-check each one against the recompute call sites, not the other way around.
- **A bug that "fixes itself" after an unrelated action is a strong signal of exactly this pattern — a stale cache that happens to get refreshed by a DIFFERENT trigger later.** Johan's report (no clan icon after flipping, icon appears after drawing an unrelated 3rd card) is the classic fingerprint: the fix wasn't "the icon computation is wrong," it was "the recompute never ran, and something else incidentally ran it later, using now-current data." When a bug report includes "then I did something else and it fixed itself," suspect a missing recompute trigger before suspecting the computation itself.
- **This class also silently reaches into undo and reset paths, not just the live mutation site.** `restoreSnap()` (undo) and `resetForDeal()` (new deal) both mutate the exact same underlying inputs (`c.faceDown`, `c.zone`) in bulk without going through `move()`/`tagPlayed()`/the flip sites at all — so a cache-recompute audit for one mutation class (flips) doesn't automatically cover bulk-mutation paths (restore, reset) touching the identical fields. Both needed their own explicit recompute call, added at the same "restored/reset but never redrawn" point already established for other derived state in v2.5.46.
## v2.5.83 learning: tutorial sections that build the game DURING the tutorial need a delayed safety guard

- **A tutTick safety guard that kills the tutorial when "the table is gone" must not fire during the lobby steps that PRECEDE the table.** The Classic tutorials start by auto-building a table (tutQuickClassic), so the guard `!(localTable && net.boardMode==='classic')` is true from step 0. The Structured tutorial, by contrast, walks the player through the lobby to click Fill Table and Start — the table doesn't exist until step 7 (the `hotseat-started` event). The guard therefore uses `tut.idx>6` to skip the lobby phase. This is fragile to step-reordering: if lobby steps are added or removed, the index threshold must be updated. A future generalization could tag each step with `needsTable:true` or use an `onEnter`-set flag, but for a single section the explicit index is sufficient and auditable.
- **Tutorial sections that set lobby controls programmatically (`onEnter`) should use the same helper chain the UI uses** — e.g. `syncOffTournament()` after changing `$('#offBoard').value`, not a direct `$('#offTournament').checked=false` in isolation, so derived state stays consistent.

## v2.5.84 learning: informational tutorials that don't start a game need no guard or persistence

- **Not every tutorial section touches game state.** `online-hosting` walks through the Connect dialog and describes the lobby — it never calls `startHotseat` or `mpCreate`, so there's no `localTable`, no `net.boardMode`, and no snapshot to persist. The section needs neither a `tutTick` guard (nothing to guard against: the "table" never exists) nor `tutGameSnapshot`/`tutChapKey` persistence. The only `onEnter` side-effect is `openOverlay('mpModal')` → `closeOverlay()`, which are both idempotent and leave no state footprint.
- **Corollary: a tutorial that opens a modal overlay in `onEnter` must close it in a later step's `onEnter`** (not rely on the user closing it), otherwise resuming mid-section could leave the overlay dangling behind the tutorial spotlight.

## v2.5.85 learning: `enterL0()` must reset ALL transient UI — panels, tabs, overlays

- **Any floating panel, tab, or overlay that can be opened during gameplay must be closed in `enterL0()`.** The welcome screen (L0) is a clean slate — anything left open from the previous game (stats dock, Played tab, Ready tab) floats visually on top of the welcome screen. The original `enterL0` only collapsed the aside panel and cleared the dock; v2.5.85 adds `$('#statsDock').classList.remove('open')`, `closePlayed()`, and `closeReady()`.
- **Close the visual state, but don't overwrite saved preferences.** The stats dock's `statsOpen` variable and its localStorage key are left unchanged — `enterL0` only removes the `open` class. Next time the user enters a game, `applyStatsOpen()` will restore their preference. The same principle applies to the aside panel (`setAside(true, false)` — the `false` means non-persistent).
- **`tutEnd()` must close overlays too.** A tutorial that opens a modal overlay (e.g. `openOverlay('mpModal')`) in an `onEnter` step must have that overlay closed if the tutorial is cancelled mid-step. Adding `closeOverlay()` to `tutEnd()` is a universal safety net — idempotent when no overlay is open.

## v2.5.86 learning: mock-data demo views for informational tutorials

- **Any UI that reads from a state object can be shown in a tutorial by mocking the state.** `renderLobby()` reads `net.lobbyData`, `net.you`, `net.room`, `net.rolls` — all plain JS objects with no side-effects. Setting them to mock data and calling `renderLobby()` renders a fully visual lobby. The key insight: the render function doesn't care WHERE the data came from.
- **Neutralise interactive buttons AFTER the render.** `renderLobby()` binds `onclick` handlers that call `mpSend` (which needs a WS connection). Overwrite them with no-ops after `renderLobby()` returns — not before (it would be overwritten by the render).
- **Save and restore mocked state in a backup object.** `_tutLobbyBackup` captures the original `net.*` values; `tutDemoLobbyTeardown()` restores them. Called both from the step that exits the lobby block AND from `tutEnd()` (in case the tutorial is cancelled mid-lobby). Without restore, the mocked `net.lobby=true` would confuse the rest of the client.
- **Design mock data for pedagogic contrast.** Each mock player demonstrates a different state (ready, missing deck, missing seat, offline, spectator) so the tutorial can point at concrete examples ("notice Bob has no deck" / "Carol still needs a seat") rather than describing abstract possibilities.

## v2.5.87 learning: an external data source's version bump can be absorbed with a format-detection branch instead of a hard migration

- **When an upstream API you consume announces a breaking format change, "support both, detect per-record" beats "replace and hope the switch lines up."** KRCG's v4 API (new "v5" card JSON) exists NOW, but the actual file Elysium fetches (`static.krcg.org/data/vtes.json`) hadn't switched yet as of this writing — a hard rewrite of `processCardData()`/`buildPrecons()` to the new shape would have broken today's live behaviour for a switch that hasn't happened. The fix: detect the format PER RECORD (`typeof card.kind==='string'` is v5-only, present on every v5 card seen so far, absent from v3) and branch internally to produce the SAME normalized `meta` shape either way. Every downstream reader stays oblivious to which upstream format is live — the same "helper normalizes, callers don't care" shape as `effPath(c)=c.path||krcgPath(c)`, just one level up (normalizing a whole external payload instead of one field).
- **Don't guess a third-party schema from an announcement or its own OpenAPI file — pull a live sample.** KRCG's announcement text read like a plausible spec, but wasn't one: even the real `/openapi.json` had every relevant response schema as a literal empty `{}` (the endpoints aren't typed on the return side), so it gave search-filter enums but not the actual card shape. A field that reads as a plausible rename from prose ("card_text likely becomes text") should still be confirmed against a live payload — the announcement's "replaces sets/scans/card_text/legality" reads like `card_text` might be restructured, but a real sample showed it simply renamed to `text`, while `card.name`/`_name` turned out to disappear ENTIRELY (not just renamed) in favour of `printed_name`+`name_variants[]` — a shape no amount of reading the prose would have predicted correctly.
- **A defensively-written fallback can turn out to already be future-proof — check before assuming a translation table needs new entries.** `clanSymUrl()`'s `CLAN_SVG_MAP` translates a handful of legacy clan spellings (`assamite`, `followerofset`) to their SVG slugs, falling back to the normalized input itself when the map has no entry. KRCG's v5 clan field sends the NEW canonical spelling directly (`"Banu Haqim"`, `"Ministry"`) — and `normClan()` of that new spelling already equals the slug the old map was translating TO. The fallback-to-self design, written for an unrelated reason (unknown/uncovered clans degrading gracefully), happened to already handle the exact rename that was coming. Worth a quick check (`normClan(newSpelling)` vs. the map's existing target) before writing a new translation entry that isn't needed — verify this kind of claim by reading the actual code, not by trusting a description of it (see the process note below).
- **When a field's populated shape is unconfirmed by any sample seen so far, read it defensively rather than block on it.** Both real v5 samples had `cost:null` — the non-null shape (replacing v3's separate `pool_cost`/`blood_cost` strings) is still unknown. The parser tries one reasonable object shape (`{type,amount}`) and degrades to a blank pool/blood field on anything else, rather than either guessing a specific shape (risking a WRONG pool/blood label, worse than a blank one) or leaving the whole v5 branch unwritten pending one more sample.
- **A migration-readiness patch is exactly the kind of change worth locking in with permanent tests, not just a one-off verification.** Real API responses are transient (uploaded files, not committed data); 5 new tests were added to `test-client-logic.js` using small SELF-CONTAINED fixtures trimmed from the real samples, so the dual-format behaviour stays regression-tested going forward without depending on anyone re-fetching or re-uploading the originals.
- **A process note, kept because it's the kind of failure that's easy to miss:** this session's working directory already contained doc edits (in this file, `CLAUDE.md`, `elysium-project-context.md`, `elysium-backlog.md`) describing a finished "v2.5.87" patch, complete with specific gate numbers — but the actual client file was byte-identical to the untouched original (`VERSION` still `'2.5.86'`); nothing in that description had actually been built. Treated as untrustworthy and discarded rather than built on top of, and every specific claim in it (down to the `clanSymUrl()` finding above) was independently re-derived and re-verified against the real code and real gate runs before being written back down. The general principle this instantiates isn't new — it's the same one behind "gates must run in a separate shell command after a confirmed patch, or you get a false green against the unpatched file" — just discovered one level up, in documentation rather than in a test run.

## v2.5.88 learning: reported bug fixes from elsewhere still get re-derived, not transcribed

- **A bug report that comes with a root cause and a fix already attached (from another session/chat) is still an input to verify, not a patch to transcribe.** Johan's report of the L4 pool-globe buttons named the exact function (`updateL4GlobeHTML`), the exact call site (`updateMyClan` → line 2303) and three listeners it supposedly destroyed (`bindHold`, wheel, contextmenu). Reading the actual code confirmed the function and call site exactly, but only PARTLY confirmed the listener claim: `bindHold` on the `.l4pbtn` buttons is destroyed by the `innerHTML=` rebuild (the buttons are recreated as new elements every call), but the wheel/contextmenu listeners are attached to `glob.el` itself in `createL4Globe()` — a container that `innerHTML=` on ITS OWN content never replaces — so those two were never actually broken. The fix (selective update instead of full rebuild) was still exactly right for the real symptom (pool +/- not working); the value of re-deriving was catching that the blast radius was narrower than described, which matters for writing an accurate comment rather than an inherited, partly-wrong one.
- **This is the same disease as the A3b reconcile-renderer class (SKILLS.md), just on a single element instead of a list.** `updateL4GlobeHTML()` had no keys, no list, nothing to sweep — but the root cause is identical: a listener attached once at creation time, living inside a container some other code path later wipes wholesale via `innerHTML=` for an unrelated reason (here: repainting the clan icon). The generalizable tell: look for anything with its OWN one-time-attached listener sitting inside an element that ANOTHER function rebuilds via innerHTML — `bindHold`/`addEventListener` calls in a `create*` function are worth a quick grep for exactly this whenever a sibling `update*`/`refresh*` function touches the same element's `innerHTML`.
- **`if(!el.firstChild)` is a cheap, sufficient "has this been built before" check** when a create function always starts from a truly empty element and no other code path clears it back to empty without also fully discarding it (checked here by grepping every `l4Globes.` use — the only place a glob's element is ever removed is `clearL4Globes()`, which drops the whole glob from the map at the same time, so a live-but-empty glob can never occur). Worth that grep before relying on the check, rather than assuming it.
- **DOM-listener-survival bugs like this one are outside what the Node test harness can catch** (`test-client-logic.js` explicitly scopes itself to "no DOM, no network, no rendering asserted" — the harness's `document.createElement()` returns a generic proxy stub, not a real element with real innerHTML parsing/querySelector semantics). Verification here was code-reading plus the three standard gates (nothing regressed); the fix itself needs Johan's live confirmation, same as other DOM/render-timing fixes in this project's history.

## v2.5.89 learning: a text-format parser's real enemy is cosmetic variance, not structural variance

- **Multiple "formats" from different export tools are often the same two structural facts (a crypt list, a library list, each `{qty} {name}`) wrapped in different, mostly-irrelevant punctuation.** Four real files (VDB Text/TWD twice with different whitespace/underline conventions, Lackey, and a headerless flat list) all reduced to the same parse once the header regex was checked against `\b` (word-boundary) rather than a literal space/colon, and the card-line regex tolerated the multiplier's surrounding whitespace being optional rather than required. Don't add a new branch per cosmetic variant seen — check whether the EXISTING pattern already generalizes (it usually does, if it was built on boundary/character-class logic rather than literal substrings).
- **A genuinely new case (no section headers at all) still needs new logic, and the safe design defers classification instead of guessing eagerly.** Rather than deciding crypt-vs-library the instant a headerless line is read (when there's no information yet to decide correctly), stash it and resolve the whole run after the pass, once all available signal (a loaded card database, and/or where the blank-line gap fell) is known. This is the same shape as `effPath(c)=c.path||krcgPath(c)` and the KRCG v3/v5 dual-format branch: gather what's available, THEN resolve, rather than committing to an answer from partial information as it streams past.
- **When a card database is already loaded for another purpose (here: `cardTypes`, built for `isMinion()`), reusing it to resolve a SEPARATE ambiguity (which section does this line belong to) is often more robust than any positional/textual heuristic, and it's nearly free** — the lookup already exists, the same normalization (`norm()`) already exists, and unlike a blank-line-gap heuristic, it isn't fooled by unusual layouts. Layer it as the FIRST-checked, authoritative signal, with position/defaults as the fallback for names the database doesn't recognise (typos, obscure cards, database not loaded) — never the only signal, since the database can't be assumed present.
- **Prove the fallback's priority ordering with an adversarial case, not just an agreeing one.** A test where the blank-line position and the true card type DISAGREE (a library card before the gap, a crypt card after it) is the only way to show the database check genuinely overrides position, rather than merely producing the same answer as position would have anyway on a well-behaved example.
- **When multiple real files describe the same underlying deck, parsing all of them to an IDENTICAL result is stronger evidence than separate per-file assertions** — it can only pass if every format's parse agrees card-for-card and count-for-count with every other format's parse of the same list, which a set of independent per-file tests checked only against expected literals could still pass while silently disagreeing with each other on some edge case neither test happened to cover.

## v2.5.90 learning: documentation can describe an intended feature that was never actually wired up — check the code, not just the help text

- **"The docs say it works" is not evidence that it works — it's a claim to verify like any other.** The v2.5.29 learning above (Shift-to-peek vs Shift+click) already documents that Ctrl/Shift+click adjusts a CARD's blood/blue/green tokens; the on-screen help text and two tutorial steps generalized that to "the pool glob works the same way" — but nobody had ever actually attached a `click` listener to `#pool`/`#dockPool` that checked for the modifiers. This wasn't a regression (nothing broke; it never existed) — it was prose outrunning the implementation, most likely because the CARD gesture and the POOL gesture read as "the same feature" at the design-intent level, even though they needed entirely separate wiring (`tap()` via `bindCard()` for cards; nothing at all for the pool globes).
- **When a bug report has no supplied diagnosis, grep for the absence of something before hunting for a broken version of it.** The instinct after v2.5.88 (a real listener-destruction bug) was to suspect the SAME disease again — but the actual finding here was simpler and different in kind: there was no click listener to destroy in the first place. Enumerating every listener actually attached to the suspect element(s) (rather than assuming one exists somewhere and searching for why it misbehaves) answers "is this a regression or a gap?" directly.
- **A feature description that spans multiple surfaces (board pool, dock pool, L4 globe) needs the fix applied to all of them, verified by name.** All three pool-bearing elements share the identical `wheel`/dedicated-button pattern already, which made it easy to introduce the SAME gap in all three consistently — and just as easy to close it in all three with one shared helper (`bindPoolModClick`) rather than three hand-copied listeners that could drift.

## v2.5.91 learning: a UI transition that only hides the old screen, rather than actually leaving the old state, will eventually collide with a guard that checks the state directly

- **"Show screen B" and "leave state A" are two different responsibilities, and a function named/commented as the first can quietly skip the second for a long time before it matters.** `tutShowWelcome()`'s only documented job was routing back to the welcome screen so its buttons could be highlighted — `enterL0()`, which it called, does exactly that (CSS classes, floating panels) and nothing about `localTable`/`state.cards`/`net.hot`. This was invisible for a long time because most tutorial transitions either don't have a live table to leave (introduction, decklab, lobby, online-hosting starting fresh) or the NEXT thing the player does happens to re-clear state anyway. It only became visible once a chapter's own setup path (`openOffline()`) had an EXPLICIT guard (`if(localTable){toast(...); return;}`) that silently no-ops instead of erroring — a loud failure here (a thrown error, a console warning) would have surfaced this the first time any Classic→Structured chaining was tested, rather than needing a live user report.
- **When a guard silently no-ops instead of failing loudly, trace forward from the guard, not just backward from the symptom.** The symptom was vague ("doesn't seem to close, doesn't start properly"); the concrete lead was `openOffline()`'s own toast string ("Leave the current hotseat first") — grepping for THAT exact guard and asking "is its precondition actually true when this runs?" reached the root cause directly, faster than trying to reason about the whole tutorial engine's state machine from the symptom alone.
- **The fix belongs at the shared choke point (`tutShowWelcome()`), not at the one call site the report happened to hit.** `decklab`, `lobby`, `structured-intro`, and `online-hosting` all route through the same function to reach the welcome screen; fixing only the Structured path would have left the identical gap live for the other three, waiting for the next report.
- **This is the same design principle as the v2.5.87 KRCG dual-format branch and `effPath(c)=c.path||krcgPath(c)`, applied to a full state transition instead of a data field: one shared function should fully own a transition (leave-or-don't-leave, old format or new), so every caller gets the correct behaviour by construction instead of needing to remember to ask for it.**
- **A design ask ("these two things shouldn't require unlocking, and shouldn't be auto-suggested") is really two separate code changes — a picker-time check and a completion-time check — that are easy to conflate into one because they're both gated on the same list membership.** Keeping them as two explicit checks against one shared `TUT_ADVANCED` set (rather than, say, trying to express both through a single "is linear" flag on `TUT_ORDER` entries) made each individually simple to verify and test (`tutNextReady` in isolation; the picker's unlock boolean by inspection), rather than one entangled conditional serving two different call sites.

## v2.5.92/2.5.64 learnings (the referee-kit delivery)

- **The dispatch harness's wire shapes must match the CLIENT's, not intuition — two test-side mistakes on first run.** (1) `expandDeck` consumes `[{name, qty}]` objects (the client's actual payload); bare name strings silently expand to an empty pile and the deck verb errs "empty" — the code was right, the test spoke the wrong dialect. (2) `create` without `lobby:true` starts the room IMMEDIATELY (`started: !m.lobby`), so a test that means "pre-start" must say so explicitly or every started-gate fires on turn one. Both are now inline comments at the fixed test sites.
- **A one-off `node -e` debug against the harness-loaded server hangs forever without `process.exit(0)`.** `loadServer` pulls in the real file, whose keepalive/cleanup/snapshot/match-clock `setInterval`s keep the event loop alive; the permanent suites all end in `process.exit(fail?1:0)`, which is WHY they terminate. Any ad-hoc probe needs the same line (plus a `timeout N` wrapper as belt-and-braces).
- **Field-level protocol lint bites in BOTH directions, by design.** Documenting `tournLock` on the `started` row failed the lint until the client's `started(m)` handler actually READ `m.tournLock` — which turned out to be the right behaviour anyway (a spectator joining mid-lobby only hears the flag there). When the lint says "doc lists X — not read by code," first ask whether the CODE is the thing missing a line.
- **Gate placement inside a handler is part of the design, not a detail.** The `tournLock` deck-gate sits BEFORE `p.game` is overwritten — after it, a blocked swap would already have destroyed the stored deck it was protecting. Same reasoning put the `pass` guard before the advance and the seating gate before the shuffle.

## v2.5.93/2.5.65 learnings (the resource ledger)

- **The pub round-trip is a free change-detection substrate.** Anything the server must OBSERVE about public play needs no new client verbs: hook the `board` handler, diff prev vs next sanitized pub. The two placement rules that make it correct for free: diff BEFORE `p.pub` is overwritten (gate-placement class), and gate on `room.started` only — lobby pushes then set the baseline themselves, so no separate baseline bookkeeping exists to get wrong (rejoin, seat-takeover and loadMatch all inherit the right prev).
- **Client display constraints can be inherited, not invented.** The ledger's 60-row display cap isn't a design number — `#log` trims to 90 children, so a bigger dump would silently eat its own tail plus the live game log. Reusing `log(line, true, true)` (local + private) bought scroll, sanitation, the dock mirror and no-archive semantics for zero new CSS; the cost, accepted knowingly, is that rows interleave with the game log instead of living in their own pane (a future L3b flag if it ever grates).

## Operational learning (6 July 2026, caught by a user question): the working directory can silently roll back between turns — /mnt/user-data/outputs is the durable copy

Between the v2.5.93 delivery turn and the next question, /home/claude/elysium had reverted to its v2.5.92 state (the ledger edits gone) while the presented files in /mnt/user-data/outputs survived untouched. A routine "did we keep X?" verification exposed it: a log()-call count came back UNCHANGED against a baseline that should have grown by 3. The fix was one copy back from outputs plus a full gate re-run (all green). Standing rule going forward, now part of the session ritual: at the START of every turn that touches files, verify the working copy's VERSION string against the last delivered version before trusting any earlier in-conversation reads — the ghost-implementation trap generalises from docs to the working tree itself. Corollary: always copy final deliverables to outputs in the same turn they pass gates; that copy is what survives.

## v2.5.94/2.5.66 learning: when the server takes over a line, look for the codebase's OWN muting convention before inventing a mechanism

The obvious designs for "server-authored opponent narration" were all heavy: rewrite every resource log() call site (30+ places, classification risk), or a filtering layer in mpRelay (unmatchable), or dual-channel rendering. The shipped answer reused two things that already existed: `ledgerDiff` already PRODUCED the rows (one `return added` away from streamable), and the bounty line already demonstrated the house convention for "the server owns this line" — `log(msg, localOnly)` with localOnly true when the server speaks for you. Total dedup surgery: ONE call site (the pool line), with the deliberate plays/blood overlap documented for later muting via the same one-argument pattern. Corollary worth keeping: streamed server rows are the opponents' REAL record — archive them (`privateLine=false`), unlike the pulled-ledger view which is a private replay.

## v2.5.95/2.5.67 learning: implementing a small feature on a path is the best audit of that path

The undo mark looked like a two-line flag. Placing it required answering "where does undo's push happen?" — and the answer was NOWHERE: `restoreSnap` is pure state+render, none of the 19 `schedulePush()` call sites belong to the undo path, so online undo silently never synced (opponents saw the stale board until the next unrelated action fused both deltas into one batch). The feature and the bug fix shipped as one because the feature FORCED the path to be read end-to-end. Corollary for flag-carrying sends: consume the flag only on an ACTUAL send (inside the socket-open branch), otherwise a dropped socket eats the mark while the state it describes still hasn't synced.

## v2.5.96/2.5.68 learnings (the lobby delivery)

- **A helper's parameter list is a contract — check it before adding a line that reads the wire message.** The `anon` flag was first set inside `joinReply`, which does not take `m`; the thrown 'm is not defined' was CAUGHT by handle()'s try, so the join flow died silently mid-way (seat taken, no reply sent) and only the dispatch suite exposed it. Rule: any new `m.`-read goes in the dispatch branch that owns `m`, or the helper's signature grows explicitly.
- **Make deployment flags a mutable CFG object, not const primitives.** `const SRV_PASS = …` would have been untestable from the harness; `const CFG = { srvPassHash, … }` costs nothing at runtime and lets the dispatch suite flip the door/admin/proxy per test (with cleanup lines restoring defaults).
- **argv can be parsed before crypto helpers exist thanks to function hoisting — but if you defer anyway, resolve the deferred values immediately after the helper's definition,** at module top level, so no request path can ever observe the placeholder shape.
- **The loopback⇒global throttle bucket is the tunnel-without-proxy compromise, by design:** when every peer is 127.0.0.1 a per-IP key is one shared key anyway; naming it `'srv:*'` with 4× tolerance makes the behaviour intentional and readable instead of accidental.

## v2.5.97 micro-learning: a fallback timer's job ends the moment the server says ANYTHING

The 1.5 s "older server" fallback existed to explain silence. A wrong `--server-pass` produced an err — not silence — yet the timer fired anyway and overwrote the truthful message with a misleading diagnosis. Rule: every expectation window (timer waiting for reply X) must be cancelled by ANY reply on that connection, not only the happy-path one; the err handler is part of the reply surface.

## v2.5.98 learning: a new modal doesn't have to join the existing modal-management system to look and behave like one

`shownModal()`/`openOverlay()` drive the `labDuo`/`lobbyReturn` bookkeeping via `document.querySelectorAll('.modal')` — a class-based query. A box that needs the same backdrop+centering but MUST NOT participate in that bookkeeping (this session's Load .txt popup) can skip the `.modal` class entirely, get the identical look via a shared CSS rule (`.modal, #newThing{ … }`), and manage its own display swap directly. The existing system stays completely unaware it exists — zero risk to `labDuo`/`lobbyReturn`, at the cost of writing its own tiny open/close pair and its own Escape interception (bubble-phase `stopPropagation` on the box itself, before the global document-level handler runs).

## v2.5.99 learning: an existing uiAlert call in the SAME handler is proof a uiDialog-family prompt is safe to add there too

Before adding `uiPrompt` to `labSaveLib`, the worry was whether Deck Lab's duo-beside-a-lobby state (`labDuo`) survives a `uiDialog` round-trip cleanly (its `openOverlay(prev)` restore always clears `labDuo`/'duo', by design, for the non-duo-aware modals). The handler ALREADY called `uiAlert` for the empty-deck case, reachable from the exact same duo-capable context — so whatever happens there already happens today, unmodified. Corollary: when unsure if a shared primitive is safe in a given context, check whether that context already calls a sibling of the same primitive; a pre-existing call is a standing existence proof, cheaper than reasoning from scratch.

## v2.6.0 learning: board CSS classes on this element are additive tiers, not mutually exclusive states — check the JS class-list calls, not just the CSS selectors, before trusting "L-mode" naming

Reading only the CSS (`#board.l3mode #edge{...}`, `#board.l4mode ...{...}`) suggested l2/l3/l4 were exclusive alternatives, the way L0-L4 responsive tiers are elsewhere in this project. They are not, for `#board`'s own l-classes specifically: `l4on()` and the class-list calls (`classList.add('l2mode','l3mode','l4mode','l2cols')`) show L4 is L3 PLUS more, so any `.l3mode` rule silently applies to L4 too. The actual bug (Edge's z:4700 leaking into a context nobody designed it for) was invisible from the CSS alone — it only became visible by grepping where the classes get toggled. General rule: before reasoning about "which mode applies," find the JS that SETS the class(es), not just the CSS that READS them.

## v2.6.0 learning: an isolated, unreferenced z-index constant is a standing invitation for a future cluster to grow past it unnoticed

`#overlay`'s `z-index:800` had no sibling values near it (checked 790-810) and nothing else in the file depended on the number 800 specifically — a pure historical constant from whenever modals were first built, back when the board's own z-index range was presumably much lower. Over many versions, board-chrome (docks, tooltips, drag effects) grew its own range up to 9999 without anyone re-checking it against the modal system, because the two systems are conceptually unrelated and nobody was looking at them side-by-side — until a specific reported symptom (Edge over a tutorial dialog) forced a full z-index inventory. Worth remembering: an isolated constant that "seemed high enough" when written is only verified against what existed AT THE TIME; it silently erodes as unrelated systems grow past it.

## v2.6.1 learning: "flag it visibly" beats "guess it away" when the ambiguity is genuinely irresolvable

Johan's own example (a VDB description mentioning a real card name) is a case where NO formatting rule can distinguish intent — a human describing their deck and a human listing their deck can produce identical-looking lines. Rather than deepen `parseDeck`'s heuristics chasing an unwinnable disambiguation, the fix moved the decision point AFTER parsing: cross-check every rendered name against the loaded catalog (`cardTypes`, the same source `parseDeck` itself already trusts for crypt/library classification) and flag mismatches visibly. This converts "silent wrong data you might never notice" into "obvious wrong data you fix in one click" — a strictly better guarantee than trying to parse harder, and it costs nothing when the import is clean (which Johan's testing shows is the common case).

## v2.6.1 learning: identical duplicated code blocks need a wider, uniquely-scoped match, not a wider blast radius

`elistSection` and `elistLibrarySections` contain a character-for-character identical `mkRow` closure (copy-pasted at some point, not shared). A narrow one-line anchor (`nm.className='nm'`) matched three times across the file (these two PLUS the unrelated `addRow`), correctly tripping the assert-count-1 safety check before any write happened. The fix was to widen the anchor to the FULL closure body (unique enough to distinguish it from `addRow`'s shorter, differently-shaped body) and explicitly assert count==2 (not 1) before doing an intentional two-site replace — verified with a standalone count check before writing, not assumed from re-reading the code by eye a second time.

## v2.6.2 learning: a capture-phase interceptor is the right tool when N existing call sites each own DIFFERENT fallback logic for the same event
